</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Approximate distribution of native German speakers (assuming a rounded total of 95 million) worldwide.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#282828; color:white;"> </span> Germany (78.3%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Yellow; color:black;"> </span> Austria (8.4%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF0000; color:black;"> </span> Switzerland (5.6%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#008751; color:black;"> </span> Brazil (3.2%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#85C1E9; color:black;"> </span> Italy (South Tyrol) (0.4%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:Grey; color:black;"> </span> Other (4.1%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/German_diaspora" title="German diaspora">German diaspora</a>, as well as the popularity of German taught as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA-2015_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MLA-2015-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat-2016_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-eurostat-2016-25">[22]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_German_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of German speakers">geographical distribution of German speakers</a> (or "Germanophones") spans all inhabited continents.
</p><p>However, an exact, global number of native German speakers is complicated by the existence of several varieties whose status as separate "languages" or "dialects" is disputed for political and linguistic reasons, including quantitatively strong varieties like certain forms of <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoossens198327_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoossens198327-3">[3]</a></sup> With the inclusion or exclusion of certain varieties, it is estimated that approximately 90–95 million people speak German as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisSimonsFennig2015_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisSimonsFennig2015-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2020)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartenSauer20057_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartenSauer20057-27">[24]</a></sup> 10–25<span class="nowrap"> </span>million speak it as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisSimonsFennig2015_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisSimonsFennig2015-26">[23]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2020)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and 75–100<span class="nowrap"> </span>million as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup> This would imply the existence of approximately 175–220<span class="nowrap"> </span>million German speakers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statista_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Statista-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="769" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg/220px-Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg/330px-Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg/440px-Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Legal_status_of_German_in_Europe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The German language in Europe: <small><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffcc00; color:black;"> </span> <b>German</b> <i><b><a href="/wiki/Sprachraum" title="Sprachraum">Sprachraum</a></b></i>: German is the official language (<i>de jure</i> or <i>de facto</i>) and first language of the majority of the population</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d98575; color:black;"> </span> German is a co-official language but not the first language of the majority of the population</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#7373d9; color:black;"> </span> German (or a German dialect) is a legally recognized minority language (squares: geographic distribution too dispersed/small for map scale)</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#30efe3; color:black;"> </span> German (or a variety of German) is spoken by a sizeable minority but has no legal recognition</div></small></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Languages_Austria.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Languages_Austria.svg/220px-Languages_Austria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Languages_Austria.svg/330px-Languages_Austria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Languages_Austria.svg/440px-Languages_Austria.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Languages_Austria.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD700; color:black;"> </span> Most of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> lies in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bavarian_dialects" title="Bavarian dialects">Bavarian</a> dialect area; only the very west of the country is</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF4500; color:black;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alemannic_dialects" title="Alemannic dialects">Alemannic</a>-speaking.</div><i>Map shows Austria and <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">South Tyrol</a>, Italy.</i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Languages_CH.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="905" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_Languages_CH.png/220px-Map_Languages_CH.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_Languages_CH.png/330px-Map_Languages_CH.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_Languages_CH.png/440px-Map_Languages_CH.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Languages_CH.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF8C00; color:black;"> </span> (<a href="/wiki/Swiss_German" title="Swiss German">Swiss</a>) German is one of four national languages of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, and it is spoken in <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Switzerland" title="List of cities in Switzerland">seven of the country's ten largest cities</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moselfrankisch.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="527" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Moselfrankisch.png/200px-Moselfrankisch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Moselfrankisch.png/300px-Moselfrankisch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Moselfrankisch.png/400px-Moselfrankisch.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moselfrankisch.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#9999FF; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> lies in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moselle_Franconian" title="Moselle Franconian">Moselle Franconian</a> dialect area.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="261" data-file-width="319" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg/220px-BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg/330px-BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg/440px-BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BelgieGemeenschappenkaart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0000FF; color:white;"> </span> In <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, German is spoken in the country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-speaking_Community" title="German-speaking Community">German-speaking Community</a>, in the very east of the country.</div></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, about 90<span class="nowrap"> </span>million people, or 16% of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s population, spoke German as their mother tongue, making it the second most widely spoken language on the continent after Russian and the second biggest language in terms of overall speakers (after English), as well as the most spoken native language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="German_Sprachraum">German Sprachraum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: German Sprachraum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The area in central Europe where the majority of the population speaks German as a first language and has German as a (co-)official language is called the "German <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Sprachraum" title="German Sprachraum">Sprachraum</a></i>". German is the official language of the following countries:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a></li>
<li>17 cantons of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a></li></ul>
<p>German is a co-official language of the following countries:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (as majority language only in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-speaking_Community" title="German-speaking Community">German-speaking Community</a>, which represents 0.7% of the Belgian population)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, along with French and Luxembourgish</li>
<li>Switzerland, co-official at the federal level with French, Italian, and Romansh, and at the local level in four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_cantons_of_Switzerland" title="List of cantons of Switzerland">cantons</a>: <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Bern" title="Canton of Bern">Bern</a> (with French), <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Fribourg" title="Canton of Fribourg">Fribourg</a> (with French), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Grisons" title="Canton of Grisons">Grisons</a> (with Italian and Romansh) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Valais" title="Canton of Valais">Valais</a> (with French)</li>
<li>Italy, (as majority language only in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_Province_of_South_Tyrol" title="Autonomous Province of South Tyrol">Autonomous Province of South Tyrol</a>, which represents 0.6% of the Italian population)</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_the_German_Sprachraum">Outside the German Sprachraum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Outside the German Sprachraum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_(1944%E2%80%931950)" title="Flight and expulsion of Germans (1944–1950)">expulsions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Germans" title="Persecution of Germans">(forced) assimilation</a> after the two <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">World wars</a> greatly diminished them, minority communities of mostly bilingual German native speakers exist in areas both adjacent to and detached from the Sprachraum.
</p><p>Within Europe, German is a recognized minority language in the following countries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charter-ratifications_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-charter-ratifications-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/Germans_in_the_Czech_Republic" title="Germans in the Czech Republic">Germans in the Czech Republic</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (see also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Schleswig_Germans" title="North Schleswig Germans">North Schleswig Germans</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Hungary" title="Germans of Hungary">Germans of Hungary</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/German_minority_in_Poland" title="German minority in Poland">German minority in Poland</a>; German is an <a href="/wiki/Bilingual_communes_in_Poland" title="Bilingual communes in Poland">auxiliary and co-official language in 31 communes</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/Germans_of_Romania" title="Germans of Romania">Germans of Romania</a>)</li>
<li>Russia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> (see also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Germans_in_Russia,_Ukraine_and_the_Soviet_Union" title="History of Germans in Russia, Ukraine and the Soviet Union">Germans in Russia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> (see also: <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Germans" title="Carpathian Germans">Carpathian Germans</a>)</li></ul>
<p>In France, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_German" title="High German">High German</a> varieties of <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">Alsatian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moselle_Franconian" title="Moselle Franconian">Moselle Franconian</a> are identified as "<a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional languages</a>", but the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> of 1998 has not yet been ratified by the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cameroon">Cameroon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cameroon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Kamerun" title="Kamerun">colony</a> of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> from 1884 to 1916. German has nowadays almost entirely yielded to its two successors, French and English. However, as a foreign language subject German still enjoys huge popularity among pupils and students, with 300,000 people learning or speaking German in Cameroon in 2010. Today Cameroon is one of the African countries with the highest number of people with knowledge of German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Namibia">Namibia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Namibia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_language_in_Namibia" title="German language in Namibia">German language in Namibia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="605" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg/180px-WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg/270px-WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg/360px-WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WindhoekBaeckereiCarstensen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bilingual German-English sign at a bakery in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, where German is a national language.</div></div></div>
<p>Namibia also was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_South-West_Africa" title="German South-West Africa">colony</a> of the German Empire, from 1884 to 1915. About 30,000 people still speak German as a native tongue today, mostly <a href="/wiki/German_Namibians" title="German Namibians">descendants of German colonial settlers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmh-2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-nmh-2007-34">[31]</a></sup> The period of German colonialism in Namibia also led to the evolution of a Standard German-based <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> language called "<a href="/wiki/Namibian_Black_German" title="Namibian Black German">Namibian Black German</a>", which became a second language for parts of the indigenous population. Although it is nearly extinct today, some older Namibians still have some knowledge of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeumert2003561–613_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeumert2003561–613-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>German remained a <i>de facto</i> official language of Namibia after the end of German colonial rule alongside English and <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, and had <i>de jure</i> co-official status from 1984 until its independence from South Africa in 1990. However, the Namibian government perceived Afrikaans and German as symbols of <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> and colonialism, and decided English would be the sole official language upon independence, stating that it was a "neutral" language as there were virtually no English native speakers in Namibia at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmh-2007_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-nmh-2007-34">[31]</a></sup> German, Afrikaans, and several indigenous languages thus became "national languages" by law, identifying them as elements of the cultural heritage of the nation and ensuring that the state acknowledged and supported their presence in the country.
</p><p>Today, Namibia is considered to be the only German-speaking country outside of the <i>Sprachraum</i> in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> German is used in a wide variety of spheres throughout the country, especially in business, tourism, and public signage, as well as in education, churches (most notably the German-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_Namibia_(GELK)" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in Namibia (GELK)">Evangelical Lutheran Church in Namibia (GELK)</a>), other cultural spheres such as music, and media (such as German language radio programs by the <a href="/wiki/Namibian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Namibian Broadcasting Corporation">Namibian Broadcasting Corporation</a>). The <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_Zeitung_(Namibia)" title="Allgemeine Zeitung (Namibia)">Allgemeine Zeitung</a></i></span> is one of the three biggest newspapers in Namibia and the only German-language daily in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmh-2007_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-nmh-2007-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An estimated 12,000 people speak German or a German variety as a first language in South Africa, mostly originating from different waves of immigration during the 19th and 20th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L1eur_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-L1eur-37">[34]</a></sup> One of the largest communities consists of the speakers of "Nataler Deutsch",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> a variety of <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> concentrated in and around <a href="/wiki/Wartburg,_KwaZulu-Natal" title="Wartburg, KwaZulu-Natal">Wartburg</a>. The South African constitution identifies German as a "commonly used" language and the <a href="/wiki/Pan_South_African_Language_Board" title="Pan South African Language Board">Pan South African Language Board</a> is obligated to promote and ensure respect for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/German_language_in_the_United_States" title="German language in the United States">German language in the United States</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">Pennsylvania German language</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plautdietsch" title="Plautdietsch">Plautdietsch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hutterite_German" title="Hutterite German">Hutterite German</a></div>
<p>In the United States, German is the fifth most spoken language in terms of native and second language speakers after English, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_United_States" title="Spanish language in the United States">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_the_United_States" title="French language in the United States">French</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_language_in_the_United_States" title="Chinese language in the United States">Chinese</a> (with figures for <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> combined), with over 1 million total speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Census_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-US_Census-40">[37]</a></sup> In the states of <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, German is the most common language spoken at home after English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blatt_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blatt-41">[38]</a></sup> As a legacy of significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German immigration to the country</a>, German geographical names can be found throughout the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest region</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/New_Ulm,_Minnesota" title="New Ulm, Minnesota">New Ulm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismarck,_North_Dakota" title="Bismarck, North Dakota">Bismarck</a> (North Dakota's state capital), plus many other regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of German varieties have developed in the country and are still spoken today, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_German_language" title="Pennsylvania German language">Pennsylvania German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas_German" title="Texas German">Texas German</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_German" title="Brazilian German">Brazilian German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonia_Tovar_dialect" title="Colonia Tovar dialect">Colonia Tovar dialect</a></div>
<p>In Brazil, the largest concentrations of German speakers are in the states of <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">Rio Grande do Sul</a> (where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riograndenser_Hunsr%C3%BCckisch" title="Riograndenser Hunsrückisch">Riograndenser Hunsrückisch</a> developed), <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">Santa Catarina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">Espírito Santo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipol_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipol-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>German dialects (namely <a href="/wiki/Hunsrik" title="Hunsrik">Hunsrik</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Pomeranian_dialect" title="East Pomeranian dialect">East Pomeranian</a>) are recognized languages in the following municipalities in Brazil: 
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">Espírito Santo</a> (statewide cultural language):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES-legislation_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ES-legislation-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Domingos_Martins" title="Domingos Martins">Domingos Martins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laranja_da_Terra" title="Laranja da Terra">Laranja da Terra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pancas,_Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Pancas, Espírito Santo">Pancas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_de_Jetib%C3%A1" title="Santa Maria de Jetibá">Santa Maria de Jetibá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vila_Pav%C3%A3o" title="Vila Pavão">Vila Pavão</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">Rio Grande do Sul</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riograndenser_Hunsr%C3%BCckisch_German" title="Riograndenser Hunsrückisch German">Riograndenser Hunsrückisch German</a> is a designated cultural language in the state):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_do_Herval" title="Santa Maria do Herval">Santa Maria do Herval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cangu%C3%A7u" title="Canguçu">Canguçu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">Santa Catarina</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipol_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipol-43">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B4nio_Carlos,_Santa_Catarina" title="Antônio Carlos, Santa Catarina">Antônio Carlos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomerode" title="Pomerode">Pomerode</a> (standard German recognized)</li></ul>
<p>Small concentrations of German-speakers and their descendants are also found in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L1eur_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-L1eur-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Australia, the state of <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> experienced a pronounced wave of Prussian immigration in the 1840s (particularly from <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a> region). With the prolonged isolation from other German speakers and contact with <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a>, a unique dialect known as <a href="/wiki/Barossa_German" title="Barossa German">Barossa German</a> developed, spoken predominantly in the <a href="/wiki/Barossa_Valley" title="Barossa Valley">Barossa Valley</a> near <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>. Usage of German sharply declined with the advent of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, due to the prevailing anti-German sentiment in the population and related government action. It continued to be used as a first language into the 20th century, but its use is now limited to a few older speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of the 2013 census, 36,642 people in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> spoke German, mostly descendants of a small wave of 19th century German immigrants, making it the third most spoken European language after English and French and overall the ninth most spoken language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>A German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-based_creole_languages" title="German-based creole languages">creole</a> named <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Unserdeutsch" title="Unserdeutsch">Unserdeutsch</a></i></span> was historically spoken in the former German colony of <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">German New Guinea</a>, modern day <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>. It is at a high risk of extinction, with only about 100 speakers remaining, and a topic of interest among linguists seeking to revive interest in the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolm1989616_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolm1989616-50">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_foreign_language">As a foreign language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: As a foreign language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:German_foreign_language_EU.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="803" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/220px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/330px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/German_foreign_language_EU.jpg/440px-German_foreign_language_EU.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:German_foreign_language_EU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Self reported knowledge of German as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a> in the EU member states (+<a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>), in per cent of the adult population (+15), 2005</div></div></div>
<p>Like English, French, and Spanish, German has become a standard foreign language throughout the world, especially in the Western World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goethe-institut_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-goethe-institut-51">[48]</a></sup> German ranks second on par with French among the best known foreign languages in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) after English,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levada2008_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levada2008-52">[49]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup> In terms of student numbers across all levels of education, German ranks third in the EU (after English and French)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat-2016_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-eurostat-2016-25">[22]</a></sup> and in the United States (after Spanish and French).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA-2015_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-MLA-2015-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> In 2020, approximately 15.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>million people were enrolled in learning German across all levels of education worldwide. This number has decreased from a peak of 20.1<span class="nowrap"> </span>million in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DW_survey_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-DW_survey-54">[51]</a></sup> Within the EU, not counting countries where it is an official language, German as a foreign language is most popular in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, namely the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurobarometer_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-eurobarometer-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurostat_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-eurostat-55">[52]</a></sup> German was once, and to some extent still is, a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> in those parts of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Polenz1999192–94,_96_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Polenz1999192–94,_96-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_German_phonology" title="Standard German phonology">Standard German phonology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With around 22 to 26 phonemes, the German consonant system has an average number of consonants in comparison with other languages. One of the more noteworthy ones is the unusual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant#Non-sibilant_affricates" title="Affricate consonant">affricate</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pf/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_German_phonology#Consonants" title="Standard German phonology">Standard German phonology § Consonants</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_High_German">Standard High German</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Standard High German">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg/220px-Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg/330px-Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg/440px-Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_German_EU_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Selfreported knowledge of German within the nations of the European Union</div></div></div>
<p>The basis of Standard High German developed with the <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bible</a> and the chancery language spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Saxon court</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwadesh197153_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwadesh197153-58">[55]</a></sup> However, there are places where the traditional regional dialects have been replaced by new vernaculars based on Standard High German; that is the case in large stretches of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">Northern Germany</a> but also in major cities in other parts of the country. It is important to note, however, that the colloquial Standard High German differs from the formal written language, especially in grammar and syntax, in which it has been influenced by dialectal speech.
</p><p>Standard High German differs regionally among German-speaking countries in <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a> and some instances of <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">pronunciation</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>. This variation must not be confused with the variation of local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialects" title="Dialects">dialects</a>. Even though the regional varieties of Standard High German are only somewhat influenced by the local dialects, they are very distinct. Standard High German is thus considered a <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric language</a>.
</p><p>In most regions, the speakers use a continuum from more dialectal varieties to more standard varieties depending on the circumstances.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standard_varieties_of_German.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="666" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Standard_varieties_of_German.png/180px-Standard_varieties_of_German.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Standard_varieties_of_German.png/270px-Standard_varieties_of_German.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Standard_varieties_of_German.png/360px-Standard_varieties_of_German.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standard_varieties_of_German.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The national and regional standard varieties of German<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmonBickelEbnerGasser2004_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmonBickelEbnerGasser2004-59">[56]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In German <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, German <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> are distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">varieties</a> of <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard High German</a>.
The <i>varieties of Standard High German</i> refer to the different local varieties of the <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric</a> Standard High German. They differ only slightly in lexicon and phonology. In certain regions, they have replaced the traditional German dialects, especially in Northern Germany.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/German_Standard_German" title="German Standard German">German Standard German</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Standard_German" title="Austrian Standard German">Austrian Standard German</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss Standard German</a></li></ul>
<p>In the German-speaking parts of <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, mixtures of dialect and standard are very seldom used, and the use of Standard High German is largely restricted to the written language. About 11% of the Swiss residents speak Standard High German at home, but this is mainly due to German immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> This situation has been called a <i>medial <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Standard_German" title="Swiss Standard German">Swiss Standard German</a> is used in the Swiss education system, while <a href="/wiki/Austrian_German" title="Austrian German">Austrian German</a> is officially used in the Austrian education system.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_dialects" title="German dialects">German dialects</a></div>
<p>The German dialects are the traditional local varieties of the language; many of them are not <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a> with standard German, and they have great differences in <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>. If a narrow definition of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> based on mutual intelligibility is used, many German dialects are considered to be separate languages (for instance in the <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i>). However, such a point of view is unusual in German linguistics.
</p><p>The German dialect continuum is traditionally divided most broadly into <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">High German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a>, also called <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Low Saxon</a>. However, historically, High German dialects and Low Saxon/Low German dialects do not belong to the same language. Nevertheless, in today's Germany, Low Saxon/Low German is often perceived as a dialectal variation of Standard German on a functional level even by many native speakers.
</p><p>The variation among the German dialects is considerable, with often only neighbouring dialects being mutually intelligible. Some dialects are not intelligible to people who know only Standard German. However, all German dialects belong to the dialect continuum of High German and Low Saxon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_German_or_Low_Saxon">Low German or Low Saxon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Low German or Low Saxon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>. It was the predominant language in Northern Germany until the 16th century. In 1534, the <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bible</a> was published. It aimed to be understandable to a broad audience and was based mainly on <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a> varieties. The Early New High German language gained more prestige than <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> and became the language of science and literature. Around the same time, the Hanseatic League, a confederation of northern ports, lost its importance as new trade routes to Asia and the Americas were established, and the most powerful German states of that period were located in Middle and Southern Germany.
</p><p>The 18th and 19th centuries were marked by mass education in <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a> in schools. Gradually, Low German came to be politically viewed as a mere dialect spoken by the uneducated. The proportion of the population who can understand and speak it has decreased continuously since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
The major cities in the Low German area are <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>.
</p><p>Sometimes, Low Saxon and <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian" title="Low Franconian">Low Franconian</a> varieties are grouped together because both are unaffected by the High German consonant shift.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_Franconian">Low Franconian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Low Franconian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian" title="Low Franconian">Low Franconian</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/180px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/270px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png/360px-Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niederfr%C3%A4nkisch.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">Low Franconian dialects</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Germany, Low Franconian dialects are spoken in the northwest of <a href="/wiki/North_Rhine-Westphalia" title="North Rhine-Westphalia">North Rhine-Westphalia</a>, along the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Rhine" title="Lower Rhine">Lower Rhine</a>. The Low Franconian dialects spoken in Germany are referred to as Low Rhenish. In the north of the German Low Franconian language area, North Low Franconian dialects (also referred to as Cleverlands or as dialects of <a href="/wiki/South_Guelderish" title="South Guelderish">South Guelderish</a>) are spoken. The South Low Franconian and <a href="/wiki/Bergish_dialects" title="Bergish dialects">Bergish</a> dialects, which are spoken in the south of the German Low Franconian language area, are transitional dialects between Low Franconian and <a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_language" title="Ripuarian language">Ripuarian dialects</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">Low Franconian</a> dialects fall within a linguistic category used to classify a number of historical and contemporary West Germanic varieties most closely related to, and including, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch language</a>. Consequently, the vast majority of the Low Franconian dialects are spoken outside of the German language area, in the Netherlands and Belgium. During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_Period" title="Early Modern Period">Early Modern Period</a>, the Low Franconian dialects now spoken in Germany, used <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> or Early Modern Dutch as their <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dachsprache" title="Dachsprache">Dachsprache</a>. Following a 19th-century change in Prussian language policy, use of Dutch as an official and public language was forbidden; resulting in <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">Standard German</a> taking its place as the region's official language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> As a result, these dialects are now considered German dialects from a socio-linguistic point of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> Nevertheless, topologically these dialects are structurally and phonologically far more similar to Dutch, than to German and form both the smallest and most divergent dialect cluster within the contemporary German language area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENiebaum201198_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENiebaum201198-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_German">High German</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: High German">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High_German_languages" title="High German languages">High German languages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1053" data-file-width="745" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/180px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/270px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png/360px-Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mitteldeutsche_Mundarten.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">Central German</a> dialects</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/180px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/270px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png/360px-Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fr%C3%A4nkisches_Sprachgebiet.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franconian_languages" title="Franconian languages">Franconian dialects</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">Low Franconian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">Central-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_Franconian_dialects" title="Rhine Franconian dialects">Rhine Franconian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">High Franconian)</a></div></div></div>
<p>The High German dialects consist of the <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">Central German</a>, <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">High Franconian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a> dialects. The High Franconian dialects are transitional dialects between Central and Upper German. The High German varieties spoken by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jew" title="Ashkenazi Jew">Ashkenazi Jews</a> have several unique features and are considered as a separate language, <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>, written with the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_German">Central German</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Central German">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_German" title="Central German">Central German</a> dialects are spoken in Central Germany, from <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> in the west to <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6rlitz" title="Görlitz">Görlitz</a> in the east. They consist of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franconian_languages" title="Franconian languages">Franconian dialects</a> in the west (<a href="/wiki/West_Central_German" title="West Central German">West Central German</a>) and non-Franconian dialects in the east (<a href="/wiki/East_Central_German" title="East Central German">East Central German</a>). Modern Standard German is mostly based on Central German dialects.
</p><p>The Franconian, <b>West Central German</b> dialects are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Franconian_dialects" title="Central Franconian dialects">Central Franconian dialects</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ripuarian_language" title="Ripuarian language">Ripuarian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moselle_Franconian_dialects" title="Moselle Franconian dialects">Moselle Franconian</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_Franconian_dialects" title="Rhine Franconian dialects">Rhine Franconian dialects</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hessian_dialects" title="Hessian dialects">Hessian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palatine_German_language" title="Palatine German language">Palatine</a>). These dialects are considered as
</p>
<ul><li>German in Germany and Belgium</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luxembourgish" title="Luxembourgish">Luxembourgish</a> in Luxembourg</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lorraine_Franconian" title="Lorraine Franconian">Lorraine Franconian</a> (spoken in <a href="/wiki/Moselle_(department)" title="Moselle (department)">Moselle</a>) and as a Rhine Franconian variant of <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">Alsatian</a> (spoken in <a href="/wiki/Alsace_bossue" title="Alsace bossue">Alsace bossue</a> only) in France</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limburgish_language" title="Limburgish language">Limburgish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kerkrade_dialect" title="Kerkrade dialect">Kerkrade dialect</a> in the Netherlands.</li></ul>
<p>Luxembourgish as well as the <a href="/wiki/Transylvanian_Saxon_dialect" title="Transylvanian Saxon dialect">Transylvanian Saxon dialect</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a> are based on Moselle Franconian dialects. The major cities in the Franconian Central German area are <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>.
</p><p>Further east, the non-Franconian, <b>East Central German</b> dialects are spoken (<a href="/wiki/Thuringian_dialect" title="Thuringian dialect">Thuringian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper_Saxon_German" title="Upper Saxon German">Upper Saxon</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Upper_Saxon" title="North Upper Saxon">North Upper Saxon</a>-South Markish, and earlier, in the then German-speaking parts of <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a> also <a href="/wiki/Silesian_German" title="Silesian German">Silesian</a>, and in then German southern <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a> also <a href="/wiki/High_Prussian_dialect" title="High Prussian dialect">High Prussian</a>).
The major cities in the East Central German area are <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Franconian">High Franconian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: High Franconian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">High Franconian dialects</a> are transitional dialects between Central and Upper German. They consist of the <a href="/wiki/East_Franconian_German" title="East Franconian German">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Franconian_German" title="South Franconian German">South Franconian</a> dialects.
</p><p>The East Franconian dialect branch is one of the most spoken dialect branches in Germany. These dialects are spoken in the region of <a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">Franconia</a> and in the central parts of <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxon</a> <a href="/wiki/Vogtland" title="Vogtland">Vogtland</a>. Franconia consists of the <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavarian</a> districts of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Franconia" title="Upper Franconia">Upper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Franconia" title="Middle Franconia">Middle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Franconia" title="Lower Franconia">Lower Franconia</a>, the region of <a href="/wiki/South_Thuringia" title="South Thuringia">South Thuringia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a>), and the eastern parts of the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heilbronn-Franken" title="Heilbronn-Franken">Heilbronn-Franken</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tauber_Franconia" title="Tauber Franconia">Tauber Franconia</a> and Hohenlohe) in <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a>.
The major cities in the East Franconian area are <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrzburg" title="Würzburg">Würzburg</a>.
</p><p>South Franconian is mainly spoken in northern Baden-Württemberg in Germany, but also in the northeasternmost part of the region of <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> in France. In Baden-Württemberg, they are considered as dialects of German.
The major cities in the South Franconian area are <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe" title="Karlsruhe">Karlsruhe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heilbronn" title="Heilbronn">Heilbronn</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_German">Upper German</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Upper German">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1053" data-file-width="745" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png/180px-Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png/270px-Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png/360px-Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oberdeutsche_Mundarten.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a> and <a href="/wiki/High_Franconian_German" title="High Franconian German">High Franconian</a> (transitional between Central and Upper German)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Upper_German" title="Upper German">Upper German</a> dialects are the <a href="/wiki/Alemannic_German" title="Alemannic German">Alemannic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swabian_German" title="Swabian German">Swabian</a> dialects in the west and the <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_language" title="Bavarian language">Bavarian</a> dialects in the east.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Alemannic_and_Swabian">Alemannic and Swabian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Alemannic and Swabian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Alemannic dialects are spoken in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> (<a href="/wiki/High_Alemannic_German" title="High Alemannic German">High Alemannic</a> in the densely populated <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Plateau" title="Swiss Plateau">Swiss Plateau</a>, in the south also <a href="/wiki/Highest_Alemannic_German" title="Highest Alemannic German">Highest Alemannic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Low_Alemannic_German" title="Low Alemannic German">Low Alemannic</a> in <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>), Baden-Württemberg (<a href="/wiki/Swabian_German" title="Swabian German">Swabian</a> and Low Alemannic, in the southwest also High Alemannic), <a href="/wiki/Swabia_(Bavaria)" title="Swabia (Bavaria)">Bavarian Swabia</a> (Swabian, in the southwesternmost part also Low Alemannic), <a href="/wiki/Vorarlberg" title="Vorarlberg">Vorarlberg</a> (Low, High, and Highest Alemannic), <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> (Low Alemannic, in the southernmost part also High Alemannic), <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a> (High and Highest Alemannic), and in the <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">Tyrolean</a> <a href="/wiki/Reutte_District" title="Reutte District">district of Reutte</a> (Swabian). The Alemannic dialects are considered as <a href="/wiki/Alsatian_dialect" title="Alsatian dialect">Alsatian</a> in Alsace.
The major cities in the Alemannic area are <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freiburg" title="Freiburg">Freiburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucerne" title="Lucerne">Lucerne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bern" title="Bern">Bern</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Bavarian">Bavarian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Bavarian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Bavarian dialects are spoken in <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">Lower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upper_Austria" title="Upper Austria">Upper Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Styria" title="Styria">Styria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carinthia" title="Carinthia">Carinthia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salzburg_(state)" title="Salzburg (state)">Salzburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burgenland" title="Burgenland">Burgenland</a>, and in most parts of <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">Tyrol</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Upper_Bavaria" title="Upper Bavaria">Upper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Bavaria" title="Lower Bavaria">Lower Bavaria</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Upper_Palatinate" title="Upper Palatinate">Upper Palatinate</a>), <a href="/wiki/South_Tyrol" title="South Tyrol">South Tyrol</a>, southwesternmost <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a> (Southern <a href="/wiki/Vogtlandian" title="Vogtlandian">Vogtländisch</a>), and in the Swiss village of <a href="/wiki/Samnaun" title="Samnaun">Samnaun</a>.
The major cities in the Bavarian area are <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regensburg" title="Regensburg">Regensburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graz" title="Graz">Graz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolzano" title="Bolzano">Bolzano</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regiolects">Regiolects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Regiolects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Berlin_German" title="Berlin German">Berlinian</a>, the High German regiolect or dialect of <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> with Low German substrate</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Missingsch" title="Missingsch">Missingsch</a>, a Low-German-coloured variety of High German.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ruhrdeutsch" title="Ruhrdeutsch">Ruhrdeutsch</a> (Ruhr German), the High German regiolect of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruhr_area" title="Ruhr area">Ruhr area</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/German_grammar" title="German grammar">German grammar</a></div>
<p>German is a <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional language</a> with a moderate degree of <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, with three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical genders</a>; as such, there can be a large number of words derived from the same root.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_inflection">Noun inflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=German_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Noun inflection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; text-align: center">

</td>
